   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Age of Discovery
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cantino_planisphere_(1502).jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4998" data-file-width="10643" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Cantino_planisphere_%281502%29.jpg/400px-Cantino_planisphere_%281502%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Cantino_planisphere_%281502%29.jpg/600px-Cantino_planisphere_%281502%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Cantino_planisphere_%281502%29.jpg/800px-Cantino_planisphere_%281502%29.jpg 2x" width="400"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cantino_planisphere_(1502).jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Cantino_planisphere" title="Cantino planisphere">
         Cantino planisphere
        </a>
        1502, earliest surviving chart showing the explorations of
        <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">
         Columbus
        </a>
        to Central America,
        <a href="/wiki/Gaspar_Corte-Real" title="Gaspar Corte-Real">
         Corte-Real
        </a>
        to Newfoundland,
        <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">
         Gama
        </a>
        to India and
        <a href="/wiki/Pedro_%C3%81lvares_Cabral" title="Pedro lvares Cabral">
         Cabral
        </a>
        to Brazil.
        <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tordesillas" title="Treaty of Tordesillas">
         Tordesillas
        </a>
        line depicted,
        <a href="/wiki/Biblioteca_Estense" title="Biblioteca Estense">
         Biblioteca Estense
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Modena" title="Modena">
         Modena
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <b>
       Age of Discovery
      </b>
      was a period of European global exploration that started in the early 15th century with the first
      <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_discoveries" title="Portuguese discoveries">
       Portuguese discoveries
      </a>
      in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlantic" title="Atlantic">
       Atlantic
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Archipelagos" title="Archipelagos">
       archipelagos
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">
       Africa
      </a>
      , as well as the
      <a href="/wiki/Voyages_of_Christopher_Columbus" title="Voyages of Christopher Columbus">
       discovery of the Americas
      </a>
      by
      <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">
       Spain
      </a>
      in 1492, and the Portuguese
      <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama#First_voyage" title="Vasco da Gama">
       discovery of the ocean route to the East
      </a>
      in 1498, and by a series of European naval expeditions across the Atlantic and later the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pacific" title="Pacific">
       Pacific
      </a>
      , which continued until the 18th century. It is sometimes regarded as a bridge between the
      <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">
       Middle Ages
      </a>
      and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_era" title="Modern era">
       Modern era
      </a>
      , along with its contemporary
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
       Renaissance
      </a>
      movement. European overseas expansion led to the rise of
      <a href="/wiki/Colonial_empire" title="Colonial empire">
       colonial empires
      </a>
      , with the contact between the Old and New Worlds producing the
      <a href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">
       Columbian Exchange
      </a>
      : a wide transfer of plants, animals, foods, human populations (including
      <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">
       slaves
      </a>
      ),
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infectious_disease" title="Infectious disease">
       communicable diseases
      </a>
      and culture between the
      <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Hemisphere" title="Eastern Hemisphere">
       Eastern
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Western_Hemisphere" title="Western Hemisphere">
       Western
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemisphere_of_the_Earth" title="Hemisphere of the Earth">
       Hemispheres
      </a>
      . This represented one of the most-significant
      <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">
       global
      </a>
      events concerning ecology, agriculture, and culture in history.
      <a href="/wiki/Major_explorations_after_the_Age_of_Discovery" title="Major explorations after the Age of Discovery">
       European exploration
      </a>
      allowed the global mapping of the world, resulting in a new world-view and distant civilizations acknowledging each other, reaching the most remote boundaries much later.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

       <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-42">
        <a href="#External_links">
         <span class="tocnumber">
          14
         </span>
         <span class="toctext">
          External links
         </span>
        </a>
       </li>
      </ul>
     </div>
     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">
       Overview
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Overview">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>

       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The Portuguese began systematically exploring the Atlantic coast of Africa from 1418, under the sponsorship of
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Navigator" title="Henry the Navigator">
       Prince Henry
      </a>
      . In 1488
      <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeu_Dias" title="Bartolomeu Dias">
       Bartolomeu Dias
      </a>
      reached the Indian Ocean by this route. In 1492 the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ferdinand_and_Isabella" title="Ferdinand and Isabella">
       Spanish monarchs
      </a>
      funded
      <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">
       Christopher Columbus
      </a>
      's plan to sail west to reach the Indies by crossing the Atlantic. He landed on an uncharted continent, then seen by Europeans as a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_world" title="New world">
       new world
      </a>
      , the Americas. To prevent conflict between Portugal and Spain, the
      <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tordesillas" title="Treaty of Tordesillas">
       Treaty of Tordesillas
      </a>
      was signed dividing the world into two regions of exploration, where each had exclusive rights to claim newly discovered lands.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1498, a Portuguese expedition commanded by
      <a href="/wiki/Vasco_da_Gama" title="Vasco da Gama">
       Vasco da Gama
      </a>
      reached India by sailing around Africa, opening up direct trade with Asia. Soon, the Portuguese sailed further eastward, to the valuable
      <a href="/wiki/Maluku_Islands" title="Maluku Islands">
       spice islands
      </a>
      in 1512, landing in China one year later. Thus, Europe first received news of eastern and western Pacific within a one-year span around 1512. East and west exploration overlapped in 1522, when Portuguese navigator
      <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Magellan" title="Ferdinand Magellan">
       Ferdinand Magellan
      </a>
      led a Spanish expedition West, achieving the first circumnavigation of the world, while Spanish
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Conquistador" title="Conquistador">
        conquistadors
       </a>
      </i>
      explored inland the Americas, and later, some of the South Pacific islands.
     </p>
     <p>
      Since 1495, the French and English and, much later, the
      <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">
       Dutch
      </a>
      entered the race of exploration after learning of these exploits, defying the Iberian monopoly on maritime trade by searching for new routes, first to the north, and into the Pacific Ocean around South America, but eventually by following the Portuguese around Africa into the Indian Ocean; discovering Australia in 1606, New Zealand in 1642, and Hawaii in 1778. Meanwhile, from the 1580s to the 1640s Russians explored and conquered almost the whole of
      <a href="/wiki/Russian_conquest_of_Siberia" title="Russian conquest of Siberia">
       Siberia
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Background">
       Background
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Early_world_maps" title="Early world maps">
       Early world maps
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_European_exploration_of_Asia" title="Chronology of European exploration of Asia">
       Chronology of European exploration of Asia
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1100" data-file-width="1542" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg/220px-Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg/330px-Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg/440px-Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ptolemy_Cosmographia_1467_-_world_map.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy%27s_world_map" title="Ptolemy's world map">
         Ptolemy's world map
        </a>
        (2nd century) in a 15th-century reconstruction
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      European medieval knowledge about Asia beyond the reach of
      <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">
       Byzantine Empire
      </a>
      was sourced in partial reports, often obscured by legends,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      dating back from the time of the conquests of
      <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">
       Alexander the Great
      </a>
      and his successors. Another source was
      <a href="/wiki/Radhanite" title="Radhanite">
       Radhanite Jewish trade networks
      </a>
      of merchants established as go-betweens between Europe and the Muslim world during the time of the
      <a href="/wiki/Crusader_states" title="Crusader states">
       Crusader states
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1154,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geography_in_medieval_Islam" title="Geography in medieval Islam">
       Arab geographer
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_al-Idrisi" title="Muhammad al-Idrisi">
       Muhammad al-Idrisi
      </a>
      created a description of the world and
      <a href="/wiki/World_map" title="World map">
       world map
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Tabula_Rogeriana" title="Tabula Rogeriana">
       Tabula Rogeriana
      </a>
      , at the court of King
      <a href="/wiki/Roger_II_of_Sicily" title="Roger II of Sicily">
       Roger II of Sicily
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-houben_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-houben-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-harley_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-harley-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      but still Africa was only partially known to either Christians, Genoese and Venetians, or the Arab seamen, and its southern extent unknown. There were reports of great African
      <a href="/wiki/Sahara" title="Sahara">
       Sahara
      </a>
      , but the factual knowledge was limited to the Mediterranean coasts and little else since the Arab blockade of North Africa precluded exploration inland. Knowledge about the Atlantic African coast was fragmented and derived mainly from
      <a href="/wiki/Early_world_maps" title="Early world maps">
       old
      </a>
      Greek and Roman maps based on Carthaginian knowledge, including the time of
      <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">
       Roman
      </a>
      exploration of
      <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">
       Mauritania
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">
       Red Sea
      </a>
      was barely known and only trade links with the
      <a href="/wiki/Maritime_republics" title="Maritime republics">
       Maritime republics
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">
       Republic of Venice
      </a>
      especially, fostered collection of accurate maritime knowledge.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Indian Ocean trade routes were sailed by Arab traders. Between 1405 and 1421 Chinese third Ming emperor
      <a href="/wiki/Yongle_Emperor" title="Yongle Emperor">
       Yongle
      </a>
      sponsored a series of long range
      <a href="/wiki/Treasure_voyages" title="Treasure voyages">
       tributary missions
      </a>
      under the command of
      <a href="/wiki/Zheng_He" title="Zheng He">
       Zheng He
      </a>
      (Cheng Ho).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The fleets visited
      <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">
       Arabia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">
       East Africa
      </a>
      , India,
      <a href="/wiki/Maritime_Southeast_Asia" title="Maritime Southeast Asia">
       Maritime Southeast Asia
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">
       Thailand
      </a>
      . But the journeys, reported by
      <a href="/wiki/Ma_Huan" title="Ma Huan">
       Ma Huan
      </a>
      , a Muslim voyager and translator, were halted abruptly after the emperor's death
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and were not followed up, as the Chinese
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">
       Ming Dynasty
      </a>
      retreated in the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Haijin" title="Haijin">
        haijin
       </a>
      </i>
      , a policy of
      <a href="/wiki/Isolationism" title="Isolationism">
       isolationism
      </a>
      , having limited maritime trade.
     </p>
     <p>
      By 1400 a Latin translation of
      <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">
       Ptolemy
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Geographia" title="Geographia">
        Geographia
       </a>
      </i>
      reached Italy coming from Constantinople. The rediscovery of Roman geographical knowledge was a revelation,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      both for mapmaking and worldview,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      although reinforcing the idea that the Indian Ocean was landlocked.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_travel_.281241.E2.80.931438.29">
       Medieval travel (12411438)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Medieval travel (12411438)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silk_route_copy.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1866" data-file-width="2868" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Silk_route_copy.jpg/200px-Silk_route_copy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Silk_route_copy.jpg/300px-Silk_route_copy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Silk_route_copy.jpg/400px-Silk_route_copy.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silk_route_copy.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">
         Silk Road
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">
         spice trade
        </a>
        routes later blocked by the
        <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
         Ottoman Empire
        </a>
        in 1453 spurring exploration to find alternative sea routes
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3312" data-file-width="3654" height="181" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png/200px-Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png/300px-Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png/400px-Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Travels_of_Marco_Polo.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">
         Marco Polo
        </a>
        travels (12711295)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      A prelude to the Age of Discovery was a series of European expeditions crossing
      <a href="/wiki/Eurasia" title="Eurasia">
       Eurasia
      </a>
      by land in the late Middle Ages.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although the
      <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">
       Mongols
      </a>
      had threatened Europe with pillage and destruction,
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">
         <span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2013)">
          clarification needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      Mongol states also unified much of Eurasia and, from 1206 on, the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Pax_Mongolica" title="Pax Mongolica">
        Pax Mongolica
       </a>
      </i>
      allowed safe trade routes and communication lines stretching from the Middle East to China.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A series of Europeans took advantage of these to explore eastwards. Most were Italians, as trade between Europe and the Middle East was controlled mainly by the
      <a href="/wiki/Maritime_republics" title="Maritime republics">
       Maritime republics
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      The close
      <a href="/wiki/Italian_city-states" title="Italian city-states">
       Italian
      </a>
      links to the
      <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">
       Levant
      </a>
      raised great curiosity and commercial interest in countries which lay further east.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Christian embassies were sent as far as
      <a href="/wiki/Karakorum" title="Karakorum">
       Karakorum
      </a>
      during the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Syria" title="Mongol invasions of Syria">
       Mongol invasions of Syria
      </a>
      , from which they gained understanding of the world.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      The first of these travelers was
      <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_da_Pian_del_Carpine" title="Giovanni da Pian del Carpine">
       Giovanni da Pian del Carpine
      </a>
      , dispatched by
      <a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_IV" title="Pope Innocent IV">
       Pope Innocent IV
      </a>
      to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Khan" title="Great Khan">
       Great Khan
      </a>
      , who journeyed to
      <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">
       Mongolia
      </a>
      and back from 1241 to 1247.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_10-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      About the same time, Russian prince
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yaroslav_of_Vladimir" title="Yaroslav of Vladimir">
       Yaroslav of Vladimir
      </a>
      , and subsequently his sons
      <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Nevsky" title="Alexander Nevsky">
       Alexander Nevsky
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Andrey_II_of_Vladimir" title="Andrey II of Vladimir">
       Andrey II of Vladimir
      </a>
      , traveled to the Mongolian capital. Though having strong political implications, their journeys left no detailed accounts. Other travelers followed, like French
      <a href="/wiki/Andr%C3%A9_de_Longjumeau" title="Andr de Longjumeau">
       Andr de Longjumeau
      </a>
      and Flemish
      <a href="/wiki/William_of_Rubruck" title="William of Rubruck">
       William of Rubruck
      </a>
      , who reached China through Central Asia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">
       Marco Polo
      </a>
      , a Venetian merchant, dictated an account of journeys throughout Asia from 1271 to 1295, describing being a guest at the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuan_Dynasty" title="Yuan Dynasty">
       Yuan Dynasty
      </a>
      court of
      <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">
       Kublai Khan
      </a>
      in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Marco_Polo" title="The Travels of Marco Polo">
        Travels
       </a>
      </i>
      , and it was read throughout Europe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1291, in a first Atlantic exploration attempt, merchant brothers
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vadino_and_Ugolino_Vivaldi" title="Vadino and Ugolino Vivaldi">
       Vadino and Ugolino Vivaldi
      </a>
      sailed from Genoa with two galleys but disappeared off the Moroccan coast, feeding the fears of oceanic travel.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      From 1325 to 1354, a
      <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">
       Moroccan
      </a>
      scholar from
      <a href="/wiki/Tangier" title="Tangier">
       Tangier
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Battuta" title="Ibn Battuta">
       Ibn Battuta
      </a>
      , journeyed from North Africa, the Sahara desert, West Africa, Southern Europe, Eastern Europe, the corner of Africa, the Middle East and Asia, having reached China. After return, he dictated an account of his journeys to a scholar he met in Granada, the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Rihla" title="Rihla">
        Rihla
       </a>
      </i>
      ("The Journey"),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the unheralded source on his adventures. Between 1357 and 1371 a book of supposed travels compiled by
      <a href="/wiki/John_Mandeville" title="John Mandeville">
       John Mandeville
      </a>
      acquired extraordinary popularity. Despite the unreliable and often fantastical nature of its accounts it was used as a reference
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      for the East, Egypt, and the Levant in general, asserting the old belief that Jerusalem was the
      <a href="/wiki/Axis_mundi" title="Axis mundi">
       center of the world
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Following the period of
      <a href="/wiki/Timurid_relations_with_Europe" title="Timurid relations with Europe">
       Timurid relations with Europe
      </a>
      , in 1439
      <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_de%27_Conti" title="Niccol de' Conti">
       Niccol de' Conti
      </a>
      published an account of his travels as a Muslim merchant to India and Southeast Asia and, later in 14661472, Russian merchant
      <a href="/wiki/Afanasy_Nikitin" title="Afanasy Nikitin">
       Afanasy Nikitin
      </a>
      of
      <a href="/wiki/Tver" title="Tver">
       Tver
      </a>
      travelled to India, which he described in his book
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/A_Journey_Beyond_the_Three_Seas" title="A Journey Beyond the Three Seas">
        A Journey Beyond the Three Seas
       </a>
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      These overland journeys had little immediate effect. The
      <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">
       Mongol Empire
      </a>
      collapsed almost as quickly as it formed and soon the route to the east became more difficult and dangerous. The
      <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">
       Black Death
      </a>
      of the 14th century also blocked travel and trade.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The rise of the
      <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
       Ottoman Empire
      </a>
      further limited the possibilities of European overland trade.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Atlantic_Ocean_.281419.E2.80.931507.29">
       Atlantic Ocean (14191507)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Atlantic Ocean (14191507)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Maritime_history_of_Europe" title="Maritime history of Europe">
       Maritime history of Europe
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_discoveries" title="Portuguese discoveries">
       Portuguese discoveries
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="943" height="117" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png/220px-Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png/330px-Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/29/Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png/440px-Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Republik_Venedig_Handelswege01.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">
         Genoese
        </a>
        (red) and
        <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">
         Venetian
        </a>
        (green) maritime trade routes in the Mediterranean and
        <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">
         Black Sea
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      From the 8th century until the 15th century, the
      <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Venice" title="Republic of Venice">
       Republic of Venice
      </a>
      and neighboring
      <a href="/wiki/Maritime_republics" title="Maritime republics">
       maritime republics
      </a>
      held the monopoly of European trade with the Middle East. The
      <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">
       silk
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">
       spice trade
      </a>
      , involving
      <a href="/wiki/Spice" title="Spice">
       spices
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Incense" title="Incense">
       incense
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Herb" title="Herb">
       herbs
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Drug" title="Drug">
       drugs
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Opium" title="Opium">
       opium
      </a>
      , made these Mediterranean city-states phenomenally rich. Spices were among the most expensive and demanded products of the Middle Ages, as they were used in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval_medicine" title="Medieval medicine">
       medieval medicine
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      religious
      <a href="/wiki/Ritual" title="Ritual">
       rituals
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Cosmetics" title="Cosmetics">
       cosmetics
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Perfume" title="Perfume">
       perfumery
      </a>
      , as well as
      <a href="/wiki/Food_additive" title="Food additive">
       food additives
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Food_preservatives" title="Food preservatives">
       preservatives
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They were all imported from Asia and Africa. Muslim tradersmainly descendants of Arab sailors from
      <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">
       Yemen
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">
       Oman
      </a>
      dominated maritime routes throughout the Indian Ocean, tapping source regions in the Far East and shipping for trading emporiums in India, mainly
      <a href="/wiki/Kozhikode" title="Kozhikode">
       Kozhikode
      </a>
      , westward to
      <a href="/wiki/Ormus" title="Ormus">
       Ormus
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Persian_Gulf" title="Persian Gulf">
       Persian Gulf
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Jeddah" title="Jeddah">
       Jeddah
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Red_Sea" title="Red Sea">
       Red Sea
      </a>
      . From there, overland routes led to the Mediterranean coasts. Venetian merchants distributed the goods through Europe until the rise of the
      <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">
       Ottoman Empire
      </a>
      , that eventually led to the
      <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">
       fall of Constantinople
      </a>
      in 1453, barring Europeans from important combined-land-sea routes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GT-DEX-1453-09_21-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GT-DEX-1453-09-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GT-DEX-1453-10_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GT-DEX-1453-10-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Forced to reduce their activities in the Black Sea, and at war with Venice, the
      <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">
       Genoese
      </a>
      had turned to north African trade of wheat,
      <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil" title="Olive oil">
       olive oil
      </a>
      (valued also as energy source) and a search for silver and gold. Europeans had a constant
      <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Revolution#Monetary_factors" title="Commercial Revolution">
       deficit in silver and gold
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      as coin only went one way: out, spent on eastern trade that was now cut off. Several European mines were exhausted,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the lack of
      <a href="/wiki/Bullion" title="Bullion">
       bullion
      </a>
      leading to the development of a complex banking system to manage the risks in trade (the very first state bank,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Bank_of_Saint_George" title="Bank of Saint George">
        Banco di San Giorgio
       </a>
      </i>
      , was founded in 1407 at Genoa). Sailing also into the ports of
      <a href="/wiki/Bruges" title="Bruges">
       Bruges
      </a>
      (Flanders) and England, Genoese communities established then in Portugal,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      who profited from their enterprise and financial expertise.
     </p>
     <p>
      European sailing had been primarily close to land
      <a href="/wiki/Cabotage" title="Cabotage">
       cabotage
      </a>
      , guided by
      <a href="/wiki/Portolan_chart" title="Portolan chart">
       portolan charts
      </a>
      . These charts specified proven ocean routes guided by coastal landmarks: sailors departed from a known point, followed a
      <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">
       compass
      </a>
      heading, and tried to identify their location by its landmarks.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      For the first oceanic exploration Western Europeans used the
      <a href="/wiki/Compass" title="Compass">
       compass
      </a>
      , progressive new advances in
      <a href="/wiki/Cartography" title="Cartography">
       cartography
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">
       astronomy
      </a>
      . Arab navigation tools like the
      <a href="/wiki/Astrolabe" title="Astrolabe">
       astrolabe
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Quadrant_(instrument)" title="Quadrant (instrument)">
       quadrant
      </a>
      were used for
      <a href="/wiki/Celestial_navigation" title="Celestial navigation">
       celestial navigation
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Portuguese_exploration">
       Portuguese exploration
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Portuguese exploration">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_discoveries" title="Portuguese discoveries">
       Portuguese discoveries
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_exploration_of_Africa" title="European exploration of Africa">
       European exploration of Africa
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="326" data-file-width="817" height="88" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG/220px-Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG/330px-Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG/440px-Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Niger_saharan_medieval_trade_routes.PNG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Trans-Saharan_trade" title="Trans-Saharan trade">
         Saharan trade routes
        </a>
        c. 1400, with the modern
        <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">
         Niger
        </a>
        highlighted
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bartolomeu_Dias_Voyage.PNG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="801" data-file-width="642" height="274" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Bartolomeu_Dias_Voyage.PNG/220px-Bartolomeu_Dias_Voyage.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Bartolomeu_Dias_Voyage.PNG/330px-Bartolomeu_Dias_Voyage.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Bartolomeu_Dias_Voyage.PNG/440px-Bartolomeu_Dias_Voyage.PNG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bartolomeu_Dias_Voyage.PNG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Voyage of
        <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeu_Dias" title="Bartolomeu Dias">
         Bartolomeu Dias
        </a>
        (148788)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1297, with the Portuguese part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">
       reconquista
      </a>
      completed, king
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dinis_of_Portugal" title="Dinis of Portugal">
       Dinis of Portugal
      </a>
      took personal interest in exports and in 1317 he made an agreement with Genoese merchant sailor
      <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Pessanha" title="Manuel Pessanha">
       Manuel Pessanha
      </a>
      (Pesagno), appointing him first
      <a href="/wiki/Admiral" title="Admiral">
       admiral
      </a>
      of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portuguese_navy" title="Portuguese navy">
       Portuguese navy
      </a>
      , with the goal of defending the country against Muslim pirate raids.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Outbreaks of
      <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">
       bubonic plague
      </a>
      led to severe depopulation in the second half of the 14th century: only the sea offered alternatives, with most population settling in fishing and trading coastal areas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Between 1325 and 1357
      <a href="/wiki/Afonso_IV_of_Portugal" title="Afonso IV of Portugal">
       Afonso IV of Portugal
      </a>
      encouraged maritime commerce and ordered the first explorations.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">
       Canary Islands
      </a>
      , already known to Genoese, were claimed as officially discovered under patronage of the Portuguese but in 1344 Castile disputed them, expanding their rivalry into the sea.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1415,
      <a href="/wiki/Ceuta" title="Ceuta">
       Ceuta
      </a>
      was
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Ceuta" title="Battle of Ceuta">
       conquered
      </a>
      by the Portuguese aiming to control navigation of the African coast. Young prince
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Navigator" title="Henry the Navigator">
       Henry the Navigator
      </a>
      was there and became aware of profit possibilities in the
      <a href="/wiki/Trans-Saharan_trade" title="Trans-Saharan trade">
       Trans-Saharan trade
      </a>
      routes. For centuries
      <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">
       slave and gold
      </a>
      trade routes linking West Africa with the Mediterranean passed over the Western Sahara Desert, controlled by the moors of North Africa.
     </p>
     <p>
      Henry wished to know how far Muslim territories in Africa extended, hoping to bypass them and trade directly with West Africa by sea, find allies in legendary Christian lands to the south
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      like the long-lost Christian kingdom of
      <a href="/wiki/Prester_John" title="Prester John">
       Prester John
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and to probe whether it was possible to reach the
      <a href="/wiki/Indies" title="Indies">
       Indies
      </a>
      by sea, the source of the lucrative
      <a href="/wiki/Spice_trade" title="Spice trade">
       spice trade
      </a>
      . He invested in sponsoring voyages down the coast of
      <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">
       Mauritania
      </a>
      , gathering a group of merchants, shipowners and stakeholders interested in new sea lanes. Soon the Atlantic islands of
      <a href="/wiki/Madeira" title="Madeira">
       Madeira
      </a>
      (1419) and the
      <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">
       Azores
      </a>
      (1427) were reached. In particular, they were discovered by voyages launched by the command of Prince
      <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Navigator" title="Henry the Navigator">
       Henry the Navigator
      </a>
      . The expedition leader himself, who established settlements on the island of Madeira, was
      <a href="/wiki/Jo%C3%A3o_Gon%C3%A7alves_Zarco" title="Joo Gonalves Zarco">
       Joo Gonalves Zarco
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      At the time, Europeans did not know what lay beyond
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cape_Non" title="Cape Non">
       Cape Non
      </a>
      (Cape Chaunar) on the African coast, and whether it was possible to return once it was crossed.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Nautical myths warned of oceanic monsters or an edge of the world, but Prince Henry's navigation challenged such beliefs: starting in 1421, systematic sailing overcame it, reaching the difficult
      <a href="/wiki/Cape_Bojador" title="Cape Bojador">
       Cape Bojador
      </a>
      that in 1434 one of Prince Henry's captains,
      <a href="/wiki/Gil_Eanes" title="Gil Eanes">
       Gil Eanes
      </a>
      , finally passed.
     </p>
     <p>
      A major advance was the introduction of the
      <a href="/wiki/Caravel" title="Caravel">
       caravel
      </a>
      in the mid-15th century, a small ship able to sail
      <a href="/wiki/Windward_and_leeward" title="Windward and leeward">
       windward
      </a>
      more than any other in Europe at the time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Evolved from fishing ships designs, they were the first that could leave the coastal
      <a href="/wiki/Cabotage" title="Cabotage">
       cabotage
      </a>
      navigation and sail safely on the open Atlantic. For
      <a href="/wiki/Celestial_navigation" title="Celestial navigation">
       celestial navigation
      </a>
      the Portuguese used the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ephemerides" title="Ephemerides">
       Ephemerides
      </a>
      , which experienced a remarkable diffusion in the 15th century. They were astronomical charts plotting the location of the stars over a distinct period of time. Published in 1496 by the Jewish astronomer, astrologer, and mathematician
      <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Zacuto" title="Abraham Zacuto">
       Abraham Zacuto
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Almanach_Perpetuum&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Almanach Perpetuum (page does not exist)">
       Almanach Perpetuum
      </a>
      included some of these tables for the movements of stars.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These tables revolutionized navigation, allowing to calculate
      <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">
       latitude
      </a>
      . Exact
      <a href="/wiki/Longitude" title="Longitude">
       longitude
      </a>
      , however, remained elusive, and mariners struggled to determine it for centuries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Using the caravel, systematic exploration continued ever more southerly, advancing on average one degree a year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Senegal" title="Senegal">
       Senegal
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cap-Vert" title="Cap-Vert">
       Cape Verde Peninsula
      </a>
      were reached in 1445 and in 1446,
      <a href="/wiki/%C3%81lvaro_Fernandes" title="lvaro Fernandes">
       lvaro Fernandes
      </a>
      pushed on almost as far as present-day
      <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Leone" title="Sierra Leone">
       Sierra Leone
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1453 the
      <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_Constantinople" title="Fall of Constantinople">
       fall of Constantinople
      </a>
      to the hands of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottomans" title="Ottomans">
       Ottomans
      </a>
      was a blow to Christendom and the established business relations linking with the east. In 1455
      <a href="/wiki/Pope_Nicholas_V" title="Pope Nicholas V">
       Pope Nicholas V
      </a>
      issued the
      <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">
       bull
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Romanus_Pontifex" title="Romanus Pontifex">
        Romanus Pontifex
       </a>
      </i>
      reinforcing previous
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Dum_Diversas" title="Dum Diversas">
        Dum Diversas
       </a>
      </i>
      (1452), granting all lands and seas discovered beyond Cape Bojador to king
      <a href="/wiki/Afonso_V_of_Portugal" title="Afonso V of Portugal">
       Afonso V of Portugal
      </a>
      and his successors, as well as trade and conquest against Muslims and pagans, initiating a
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Mare_clausum" title="Mare clausum">
        mare clausum
       </a>
      </i>
      policy in the Atlantic.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The king, who had been inquiring of Genoese experts about a seaway to India, commissioned the
      <a href="/wiki/Fra_Mauro_map" title="Fra Mauro map">
       Fra Mauro world map
      </a>
      , which arrived in Lisbon in 1459.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1456
      <a href="/wiki/Diogo_Gomes" title="Diogo Gomes">
       Diogo Gomes
      </a>
      reached the
      <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde" title="Cape Verde">
       Cape Verde
      </a>
      archipelago. In the next decade several captains at the service of Prince Henry - including the Genoese
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antonio_da_Noli" title="Antonio da Noli">
       Antonio da Noli
      </a>
      and Venetian
      <a href="/wiki/Alvise_Cadamosto" title="Alvise Cadamosto">
       Alvise Cadamosto
      </a>
      - discovered the remaining islands which were occupied still during the 15th century. The Gulf of Guinea would be reached in the 1460s's.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caravel_Boa_Esperanca_Portugal.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="209" data-file-width="277" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Caravel_Boa_Esperanca_Portugal.jpg/220px-Caravel_Boa_Esperanca_Portugal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/Caravel_Boa_Esperanca_Portugal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/Caravel_Boa_Esperanca_Portugal.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Caravel_Boa_Esperanca_Portugal.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Replica of
        <a href="/wiki/Caravel" title="Caravel">
         caravel
        </a>
        ship introduced in mid-15th century for oceanic exploration
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Portuguese_exploration_after_Prince_Henry">
       Portuguese exploration after Prince Henry
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Portuguese exploration after Prince Henry">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h4>
     <p>
      In 1460
      <a href="/wiki/Pedro_de_Sintra" title="Pedro de Sintra">
       Pedro de Sintra
      </a>
      reached Sierra Leone. Prince Henry died in November that year after which, given the meager revenues, exploration was granted to Lisbon merchant
      <a href="/wiki/Fern%C3%A3o_Gomes" title="Ferno Gomes">
       Ferno Gomes
      </a>
      in 1469, who in exchange for the monopoly of trade in the Gulf of Guinea had to explore 100 miles (161 kilometres) each year for five years.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With his sponsorship, explorers
      <a href="/wiki/Jo%C3%A3o_de_Santar%C3%A9m" title="Joo de Santarm">
       Joo de Santarm
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pedro_Escobar" title="Pedro Escobar">
       Pedro Escobar
      </a>
      , Lopo Gonalves,
      <a href="/wiki/Fern%C3%A3o_do_P%C3%B3" title="Ferno do P">
       Ferno do P
      </a>
      , and Pedro de Sintra made it even beyond those goals. They reached the Southern Hemisphere and the islands of the Gulf of Guinea, including
      <a href="/wiki/S%C3%A3o_Tom%C3%A9_and_Pr%C3%ADncipe" title="So Tom and Prncipe">
       So Tom and Prncipe
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Elmina" title="Elmina">
       Elmina
      </a>
      on the Gold Coast in 1471. (In the Southern Hemisphere, they used the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_Cross" title="Southern Cross">
       Southern Cross
      </a>
      as the reference for celestial navigation.) There, in what came to be called the Gold Coast in what is today Ghana, a thriving alluvial gold trade was found among the natives and Arab and Berber traders.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1478 (during the War of the Castilian Succession), near the coast at Elmina was fought a large battle between a Castilian armada of 35 caravels and a Portuguese fleet for hegemony of the Guinea trade (gold, slaves, ivory and melegueta pepper). The war ended with a Portuguese naval victory followed by the official recognition by the Catholic Monarchs of Portuguese sovereignty over most of the West African territories in dispute embodied in the Treaty of Alcovas, 1479. (See entry on Elmina.) This was the first colonial war among European powers.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1481 the recently crowned
      <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_Portugal" title="John II of Portugal">
       Joo II
      </a>
      decided to build
      <a href="/wiki/Elmina_Castle" title="Elmina Castle">
       So Jorge da Mina
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Factory_(trading_post)" title="Factory (trading post)">
       factory
      </a>
      . In 1482 the
      <a href="/wiki/Congo_River" title="Congo River">
       Congo River
      </a>
      was explored by
      <a href="/wiki/Diogo_C%C3%A3o" title="Diogo Co">
       Diogo Co
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      who in 1486 continued to
      <a href="/wiki/Cape_Cross" title="Cape Cross">
       Cape Cross
      </a>
      (modern
      <a href="/wiki/Namibia" title="Namibia">
       Namibia
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      The next crucial breakthrough was in 1488, when
      <a href="/wiki/Bartolomeu_Dias" title="Bartolomeu Dias">
       Bartolomeu Dias
      </a>
      rounded the southern tip of Africa, which he named "Cape of Storms" (Cabo das Tormentas), anchoring at
      <a href="/wiki/Mossel_Bay" title="Mossel Bay">
       Mossel Bay
      </a>
      and then sailing east as far as the mouth of the
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Fish_River" title="Great Fish River">
       Great Fish River
      </a>
      , proving that the Indian Ocean was accessible from the Atlantic. Simultaneously
      <a href="/wiki/P%C3%AAro_da_Covilh%C3%A3" title="Pro da Covilh">
       Pro da Covilh
      </a>
      , sent out traveling secretly overland, had reached
      <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">
       Ethiopia
      </a>
      having collected important information about the Red Sea and Quenia coast, suggesting that a sea route to the Indies would soon be forthcoming.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Soon the cape was renamed by king
      <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_Portugal" title="John II of Portugal">
       John II of Portugal
      </a>
      "
      <a href="/wiki/Cape_of_Good_Hope" title="Cape of Good Hope">
       Cape of Good Hope
      </a>
      " (Cabo da Boa Esperana), because of the great optimism engendered by the possibility of a sea route to India, proving false the view that had existed since
      <a href="/wiki/Ptolemy" title="Ptolemy">
       Ptolemy
      </a>
      that the Indian Ocean was
      <a href="/wiki/Landlocked_country" title="Landlocked country">
       land-locked
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Spanish_exploration:_Columbus_.26_the_.22West_Indies.22">
       Spanish exploration: Columbus &amp; the "West Indies"
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title='Edit section: Spanish exploration: Columbus &amp; the "West Indies"'>
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Voyages_of_Christopher_Columbus" title="Voyages of Christopher Columbus">
       Voyages of Christopher Columbus
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">
       European colonization of the Americas
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Viajes_de_colon_en.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1149" data-file-width="1715" height="134" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Viajes_de_colon_en.svg/200px-Viajes_de_colon_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Viajes_de_colon_en.svg/300px-Viajes_de_colon_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Viajes_de_colon_en.svg/400px-Viajes_de_colon_en.svg.png 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Viajes_de_colon_en.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The four voyages of
        <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">
         Christopher Columbus
        </a>
        14921503
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Portugal's neighbouring fellow Iberian rival,
      <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Castile" title="Crown of Castile">
       Castile
      </a>
      , had begun to establish its rule over the
      <a href="/wiki/Canary_Islands" title="Canary Islands">
       Canary Islands
      </a>
      , located off the west African coast, in 1402, but then became distracted by internal Iberian politics and the repelling of Islamic invasion attempts and raids through most of the 15th century. Only late in the century, following the unification of the crowns of Castile and Aragon and the completion of the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Reconquista" title="Reconquista">
        reconquista
       </a>
      </i>
      , did an emerging modern Spain become fully committed to the search for new trade routes overseas. The
      <a href="/wiki/Crown_of_Aragon" title="Crown of Aragon">
       Crown of Aragon
      </a>
      had been an important maritime potentate in the Mediterranean, controlling territories in eastern Spain, southwestern France, major islands like
      <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">
       Sicily
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">
       Malta
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Naples" title="Kingdom of Naples">
       Kingdom of Naples
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">
       Sardinia
      </a>
      , with mainland possessions as far as Greece. In 1492 the
      <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Monarchs" title="Catholic Monarchs">
       joint rulers
      </a>
      conquered the
      <a href="/wiki/Emirate_of_Granada" title="Emirate of Granada">
       Moorish kingdom of Granada
      </a>
      , which had been providing Castile with African goods through tribute, and decided to fund
      <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">
       Christopher Columbus
      </a>
      's expedition in the hope of bypassing Portugal's monopoly on west African sea routes, to reach "the Indies" (east and south Asia) by travelling west.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Twice before, in 1485 and 1488, Columbus had presented the project to king
      <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_Portugal" title="John II of Portugal">
       John II of Portugal
      </a>
      , who rejected it.
     </p>
     <p>
      On the evening of 3 August 1492, Columbus departed from
      <a href="/wiki/Palos_de_la_Frontera" title="Palos de la Frontera">
       Palos de la Frontera
      </a>
      with three ships; one larger
      <a href="/wiki/Carrack" title="Carrack">
       carrack
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Santa_Mar%C3%ADa_(ship)" title="Santa Mara (ship)">
        Santa Mara
       </a>
      </i>
      , nicknamed
      <i>
       Gallega
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       the Galician
      </i>
      ), and two smaller
      <a href="/wiki/Caravel" title="Caravel">
       caravels
      </a>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Pinta_(ship)" title="Pinta (ship)">
        Pinta
       </a>
      </i>
      (
      <i>
       the Painted
      </i>
      ) and
      <i>
       Santa Clara
      </i>
      , nicknamed
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ni%C3%B1a" title="Nia">
        Nia
       </a>
      </i>
      . Columbus first sailed to the Canary Islands, where he restocked for what turned out to be a five-week voyage across the ocean, crossing a section of the Atlantic that became known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Sargasso_Sea" title="Sargasso Sea">
       Sargasso Sea
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      Land was sighted on 12 October 1492, and Columbus called the island (now
      <a href="/wiki/The_Bahamas" title="The Bahamas">
       The Bahamas
      </a>
      )
      <i>
       San Salvador
      </i>
      , in what he thought to be the "
      <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">
       West Indies
      </a>
      ". Columbus also explored the northeast coast of
      <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">
       Cuba
      </a>
      (landed on 28 October) and the northern coast of
      <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">
       Hispaniola
      </a>
      , by 5 December. He was received by the native
      <a href="/wiki/Cacique" title="Cacique">
       cacique
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Guacanagar%C3%ADx" title="Guacanagarx">
       Guacanagari
      </a>
      , who gave him permission to leave some of his men behind.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:TresCarabelas.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="203" data-file-width="380" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/TresCarabelas.jpg/220px-TresCarabelas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/TresCarabelas.jpg/330px-TresCarabelas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/54/TresCarabelas.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:TresCarabelas.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Replicas of
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Ni%C3%B1a" title="Nia">
          Nia
         </a>
        </i>
        ,
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Pinta_(ship)" title="Pinta (ship)">
          Pinta
         </a>
        </i>
        and
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Santa_Mar%C3%ADa_(ship)" title="Santa Mara (ship)">
          Santa Mara
         </a>
        </i>
        at
        <a href="/wiki/Palos_de_la_Frontera" title="Palos de la Frontera">
         Palos de la Frontera
        </a>
        , Spain
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Columbus left 39 men and founded the settlement of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/La_Navidad" title="La Navidad">
        La Navidad
       </a>
      </i>
      in what is now present-day
      <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">
       Haiti
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Before returning to Spain, he kidnapped some ten to twenty-five natives and took them back with him. Only seven or eight of the native
      <i>
       Indians
      </i>
      arrived in Spain alive, but they made quite an impression on Seville.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On the return, a storm forced him to dock in
      <a href="/wiki/Lisbon" title="Lisbon">
       Lisbon
      </a>
      , on 4 March 1493. After a week in Portugal, he set sail for Spain, entering the harbor of
      <a href="/wiki/Palos_de_la_Frontera" title="Palos de la Frontera">
       Palos
      </a>
      on 15 March 1493. Word of his "discovery" of new lands
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Columbus%27s_Letter_on_the_First_Voyage" title="Columbus's Letter on the First Voyage">
       rapidly spread throughout Europe
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Columbus and other Spanish explorers were initially disappointed with their discoveries unlike Africa or Asia, the Caribbean islanders had little to trade with the Spanish ships. The islands thus became the focus of colonization efforts. It was not until the continent itself was explored that Spain found the wealth it had sought.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Treaty_of_Tordesillas_.281494.29">
       Treaty of Tordesillas (1494)
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Treaty of Tordesillas (1494)">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tordesillas" title="Treaty of Tordesillas">
       Treaty of Tordesillas
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spain_and_Portugal.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="771" data-file-width="1500" height="103" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Spain_and_Portugal.png/200px-Spain_and_Portugal.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Spain_and_Portugal.png/300px-Spain_and_Portugal.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Spain_and_Portugal.png/400px-Spain_and_Portugal.png 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spain_and_Portugal.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The 1494
        <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tordesillas" title="Treaty of Tordesillas">
         Tordesilhas Treaty
        </a>
        meridian (purple) and the later
        <a href="/wiki/Maluku_Islands" title="Maluku Islands">
         Maluku Islands
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/180th_meridian" title="180th meridian">
         antimeridian
        </a>
        (green), set at the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Zaragoza_(1529)" title="Treaty of Zaragoza (1529)">
         Treaty of Zaragoza (1529)
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Shortly after Columbus arrival from the "West Indies", a division of influence became necessary to avoid conflict between Spanish and Portuguese.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      On 4 May 1493, two months after Columbus arrival, the
      <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Monarchs" title="Catholic Monarchs">
       Catholic Monarchs
      </a>
      got a
      <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">
       bull
      </a>
      (
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Inter_caetera" title="Inter caetera">
        Inter caetera
       </a>
      </i>
      ) from
      <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_VI" title="Pope Alexander VI">
       Pope Alexander VI
      </a>
      stating that all lands west and south of a pole-to-pole line 100 leagues west and south of the
      <a href="/wiki/Azores" title="Azores">
       Azores
      </a>
      or the Cape Verde Islands should belong to Spain and, later, all mainlands and islands then belonging to India. It did not mention Portugal, which could not claim newly discovered lands east of the line.
     </p>
     <p>
      King
      <a href="/wiki/John_II_of_Portugal" title="John II of Portugal">
       John II of Portugal
      </a>
      was not pleased with the arrangement, feeling that it gave him far too little landpreventing him from reaching India, his main goal. He then negotiated directly with King
      <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_II_of_Aragon" title="Ferdinand II of Aragon">
       Ferdinand
      </a>
      and Queen
      <a href="/wiki/Isabella_I_of_Castile" title="Isabella I of Castile">
       Isabella
      </a>
      of Spain to move the line west, allowing him to claim newly discovered lands east of it.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      An agreement was reached in 1494, with the
      <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Tordesillas" title="Treaty of Tordesillas">
       Treaty of Tordesillas
      </a>
      that "divided" the world between the two powers. In this treaty the Portuguese "received" everything outside Europe east of a line that ran 370
      <a href="/wiki/League_(unit)" title="League (unit)">
       leagues
      </a>
      west of the
      <a href="/wiki/Cape_Verde" title="Cape Verde">
       Cape Verde
      </a>
      islands (already Portuguese), and the islands discovered by
      <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Columbus" title="Christopher Columbus">
       Christopher Columbus
      </a>
      on his first voyage (claimed for Spain), named in the treaty as
      <a href="/wiki/Names_of_Japan#Jipangu" title="Names of Japan">
       Cipangu
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antilia" title="Antilia">
       Antilia
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">
       Cuba
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hispaniola" title="Hispaniola">
       Hispaniola
      </a>
      ), this gave them control over Africa, Asia and eastern South America (Brazil). The Spanish received everything west of this line, territory that was still almost completely unknown, and proved to be mostly the western part of the Americas plus the Pacific Ocean islands.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="A_New_World:_Americas">
       A New World: Americas
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: A New World: Americas">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Waldseemuller_map_closeup_with_America.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1945" data-file-width="1774" height="241" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Waldseemuller_map_closeup_with_America.jpg/220px-Waldseemuller_map_closeup_with_America.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Waldseemuller_map_closeup_with_America.jpg/330px-Waldseemuller_map_closeup_with_America.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Waldseemuller_map_closeup_with_America.jpg/440px-Waldseemuller_map_closeup_with_America.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Waldseemuller_map_closeup_with_America.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Detail of 1507
        <a href="/wiki/Waldseem%C3%BCller_map" title="Waldseemller map">
         Waldseemller map
        </a>
        showing the name "America" for the first time.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Very little of the divided area had actually been seen by Europeans, as it was only divided by a geographical definition rather than control on the ground. Columbus's first voyage in 1492 spurred maritime exploration and, from 1497, a number of explorers headed west.
     </p>
     <h4>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="North_America">
       North America
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: North America">
        edit
       </a>
