         Remezov Chronicle
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1643
      <a href="/wiki/Kurbat_Ivanov" title="Kurbat Ivanov">
       Kurbat Ivanov
      </a>
      led a group of Cossacks from Yakutsk to the south of the
      <a href="/wiki/Baikal_Mountains" title="Baikal Mountains">
       Baikal Mountains
      </a>
      and discovered
      <a href="/wiki/Lake_Baikal" title="Lake Baikal">
       Lake Baikal
      </a>
      , visiting its
      <a href="/wiki/Olkhon_Island" title="Olkhon Island">
       Olkhon Island
      </a>
      . Later Ivanov made the first chart and description of Baikal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121">
       <a href="#cite_note-121">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        121
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Russians_reach_the_Pacific">
       Russians reach the Pacific
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Russians reach the Pacific">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      In 1639 a group of explorers led by
      <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Moskvitin" title="Ivan Moskvitin">
       Ivan Moskvitin
      </a>
      became the first Russians to reach the Pacific Ocean and to discover the
      <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Okhotsk" title="Sea of Okhotsk">
       Sea of Okhotsk
      </a>
      , having built a winter camp on its shore at the
      <a href="/wiki/Ulya_River" title="Ulya River">
       Ulya River
      </a>
      mouth. The Cossacks learned from the locals about the large
      <a href="/wiki/Amur_River" title="Amur River">
       Amur River
      </a>
      far to the south. In 1640 they apparently sailed south, explored the south-eastern shores of the Okhotsk Sea, perhaps reaching the mouth of the
      <a href="/wiki/Amur_River" title="Amur River">
       Amur River
      </a>
      and possibly discovering the
      <a href="/wiki/Shantar_Islands" title="Shantar Islands">
       Shantar Islands
      </a>
      on their way back. Based on Moskvitin's account,
      <a href="/wiki/Kurbat_Ivanov" title="Kurbat Ivanov">
       Kurbat Ivanov
      </a>
      drew the first Russian map of the
      <a href="/wiki/Far_East" title="Far East">
       Far East
      </a>
      in 1642.
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1643,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vasily_Poyarkov" title="Vasily Poyarkov">
       Vasily Poyarkov
      </a>
      crossed the
      <a href="/wiki/Stanovoy_Range" title="Stanovoy Range">
       Stanovoy Range
      </a>
      and reached the upper
      <a href="/wiki/Zeya_River" title="Zeya River">
       Zeya River
      </a>
      in the country of the
      <a href="/wiki/Daur_people" title="Daur people">
       Daurs
      </a>
      , who were paying tribute to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manchu" title="Manchu">
       Manchu
      </a>
      Chinese. After wintering, in 1644 Poyarkov pushed down the Zeya and became the first Russian to reach the
      <a href="/wiki/Amur_River" title="Amur River">
       Amur River
      </a>
      . He sailed down the Amur and finally discovered the mouth of that great river from land. Since his Cossacks provoked the enmity of the locals behind, Poyarkov chose a different way back. They built boats and in 1645 sailed along the
      <a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Okhotsk" title="Sea of Okhotsk">
       Sea of Okhotsk
      </a>
      coast to the
      <a href="/wiki/Ulya_River" title="Ulya River">
       Ulya River
      </a>
      and spent the next winter in the huts that had been built by
      <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Moskvitin" title="Ivan Moskvitin">
       Ivan Moskvitin
      </a>
      six years earlier. In 1646 they returned to Yakutsk.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Krsk_koch.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="2112" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Krsk_koch.JPG/150px-Krsk_koch.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Krsk_koch.JPG/225px-Krsk_koch.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Krsk_koch.JPG/300px-Krsk_koch.JPG 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Krsk_koch.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        A 17th-century
        <a href="/wiki/Koch_(boat)" title="Koch (boat)">
         koch
        </a>
        in a museum in
        <a href="/wiki/Krasnoyarsk" title="Krasnoyarsk">
         Krasnoyarsk
        </a>
        . Kochi were the earliest
        <a href="/wiki/Icebreaker" title="Icebreaker">
         icebreakers
        </a>
        and were widely used by Russians in the
        <a href="/wiki/Arctic" title="Arctic">
         Arctic
        </a>
        and on
        <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">
         Siberian
        </a>
        rivers.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1644
      <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Stadukhin" title="Mikhail Stadukhin">
       Mikhail Stadukhin
      </a>
      discovered the
      <a href="/wiki/Kolyma_River" title="Kolyma River">
       Kolyma River
      </a>
      and founded
      <a href="/wiki/Srednekolymsk" title="Srednekolymsk">
       Srednekolymsk
      </a>
      . A merchant named
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fedot_Alekseyev_Popov" title="Fedot Alekseyev Popov">
       Fedot Alekseyev Popov
      </a>
      organized a further expedition eastward, and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semyon_Dezhnyov" title="Semyon Dezhnyov">
       Semyon Dezhnyov
      </a>
      became a captain of one of the
      <a href="/wiki/Koch_(boat)" title="Koch (boat)">
       kochi
      </a>
      . In 1648 they sailed from
      <a href="/wiki/Srednekolymsk" title="Srednekolymsk">
       Srednekolymsk
      </a>
      down to the Arctic and after some time they rounded
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cape_Dezhnyov" title="Cape Dezhnyov">
       Cape Dezhnyov
      </a>
      , thus becoming the first explorers to pass through the
      <a href="/wiki/Bering_Strait" title="Bering Strait">
       Bering Strait
      </a>
      and to discover
      <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_Peninsula" title="Chukchi Peninsula">
       Chukotka
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Bering_Sea" title="Bering Sea">
       Bering Sea
      </a>
      . All their kochi and most of their men (including Popov himself) were lost in storms and clashes with the natives. A small group led by Dezhnyov reached the mouth of the
      <a href="/wiki/Anadyr_River" title="Anadyr River">
       Anadyr River
      </a>
      and sailed up it in 1649, having built new boats from the wreckage. They founded
      <a href="/wiki/Anadyrsk" title="Anadyrsk">
       Anadyrsk
      </a>
      and were stranded there, until Stadukhin found them, coming from Kolyma by land.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122">
       <a href="#cite_note-122">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        122
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Subsequently Stadukhin set off south in 1651 and discovered
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penzhin_Bay" title="Penzhin Bay">
       Penzhin Bay
      </a>
      on the northern coast of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Okhotsk_Sea" title="Okhotsk Sea">
       Okhotsk Sea
      </a>
      . He also may have explored the western shores of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kamchatka" title="Kamchatka">
       Kamchatka
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In 164950
      <a href="/wiki/Yerofey_Khabarov" title="Yerofey Khabarov">
       Yerofey Khabarov
      </a>
      became the second Russian to explore the
      <a href="/wiki/Amur_River" title="Amur River">
       Amur River
      </a>
      . Through
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olyokma" title="Olyokma">
       Olyokma
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tungur" title="Tungur">
       Tungur
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Shilka_River" title="Shilka River">
       Shilka Rivers
      </a>
      he reached Amur (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dauria" title="Dauria">
       Dauria
      </a>
      ), returned to
      <a href="/wiki/Yakutsk" title="Yakutsk">
       Yakutsk
      </a>
      and then back to Amur with a larger force in 165053. This time he
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian-Manchu_border_conflicts" title="Russian-Manchu border conflicts">
       was met with armed resistance
      </a>
      . He built winter quarters at
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Albazin" title="Albazin">
       Albazin
      </a>
      , then sailed down Amur and found Achansk, which preceded the present-day
      <a href="/wiki/Khabarovsk" title="Khabarovsk">
       Khabarovsk
      </a>
      , defeating or evading large armies of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dauria" title="Dauria">
       Daurian
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manchu" title="Manchu">
       Manchu
      </a>
      Chinese and
      <a href="/wiki/Koreans" title="Koreans">
       Koreans
      </a>
      on his way. He charted the Amur in his
      <i>
       Draft of the Amur river
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123">
       <a href="#cite_note-123">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        123
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Subsequently Russians held on to the Amur Region until 1689, when by the
      <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Nerchinsk" title="Treaty of Nerchinsk">
       Treaty of Nerchinsk
      </a>
      this land was assigned to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_Chinese" title="Imperial Chinese">
       Chinese Empire
      </a>
      (it was returned, however, by the
      <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Aigun" title="Treaty of Aigun">
       Treaty of Aigun
      </a>
      in 1858).
     </p>
     <p>
      In 165965
      <a href="/wiki/Kurbat_Ivanov" title="Kurbat Ivanov">
       Kurbat Ivanov
      </a>
      was the next head of
      <a href="/wiki/Anadyrsk" title="Anadyrsk">
       Anadyrsky
      </a>
      ostrog after
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semyon_Dezhnyov" title="Semyon Dezhnyov">
       Semyon Dezhnyov
      </a>
      . In 1660 he sailed from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anadyr_Bay" title="Anadyr Bay">
       Anadyr Bay
      </a>
      to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cape_Dezhnyov" title="Cape Dezhnyov">
       Cape Dezhnyov
      </a>
      . Atop his earlier pioneering charts, Ivanov is credited with creation of the early map of
      <a href="/wiki/Chukchi_Peninsula" title="Chukchi Peninsula">
       Chukotka
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Bering_Strait" title="Bering Strait">
       Bering Strait
      </a>
      , which was the first to show on paper (very schematically) the yet undiscovered
      <a href="/wiki/Wrangel_Island" title="Wrangel Island">
       Wrangel Island
      </a>
      , both
      <a href="/wiki/Diomede_Islands" title="Diomede Islands">
       Diomede Islands
      </a>
      and Alaska, based on the data collected from the natives of Chukotka.
     </p>
     <p>
      So, by the mid-17th century, Russians established the borders of their country close to modern ones, and explored almost the whole of Siberia, except the eastern
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kamchatka" title="Kamchatka">
       Kamchatka
      </a>
      and some regions north of the Arctic Circle. The conquest of Kamchatka later would be achieved in the early 1700-s by
      <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Atlasov" title="Vladimir Atlasov">
       Vladimir Atlasov
      </a>
      , while the discovery of the Arctic coastline and Alaska would be completed by the
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Northern_Expedition" title="Great Northern Expedition">
       Great Northern Expedition
      </a>
      in 17331743.
     </p>
     <div class="hatnote">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/Major_explorations_after_the_Age_of_Discovery" title="Major explorations after the Age of Discovery">
       Major explorations after the Age of Discovery
      </a>
     </div>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Global_impact">
       Global impact
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Global impact">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">
       Columbian Exchange
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_colonialism" title="History of colonialism">
       History of colonialism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Globalization" title="Globalization">
       Globalization
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="1004" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG/220px-New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG/330px-New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG/440px-New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:New_World_Domesticated_plants.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        New World crops. Clockwise from top left: 1.
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corn" title="Corn">
         Corn
        </a>
        (Zea mays) 2.
        <a href="/wiki/Tomato" title="Tomato">
         Tomato
        </a>
        (Solanum lycopersicum) 3.
        <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">
         Potato
        </a>
        (Solanum tuberosum) 4.
        <a href="/wiki/Vanilla" title="Vanilla">
         Vanilla
        </a>
        (Vanilla) 5. Par
        <a href="/wiki/Natural_rubber" title="Natural rubber">
         rubber
        </a>
        tree (Hevea brasiliensis) 6.
        <a href="/wiki/Cocoa_bean" title="Cocoa bean">
         Cacao
        </a>
        (Theobroma cacao) 7.
        <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">
         Tobacco
        </a>
        (Nicotiana rustica)
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      European overseas expansion led to the contact between the Old and New Worlds producing the
      <a href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">
       Columbian Exchange
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mayne_2009_124-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Mayne_2009-124">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        124
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      named after Columbus. It involved the transfer of goods unique to one hemisphere to another. Europeans brought
      <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">
       cattle
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Horse" title="Horse">
       horses
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">
       sheep
      </a>
      to the New World, and from the New World Europeans received
      <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">
       tobacco
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Potato" title="Potato">
       potatoes
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">
       maize
      </a>
      . Other items becoming important in global trade were the
      <a href="/wiki/Sugarcane" title="Sugarcane">
       sugarcane
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">
       cotton
      </a>
      crops of the Americas, and the gold and silver brought from the Americas not only to Europe but elsewhere in the Old World.
     </p>
     <p>
      The new trans-oceanic links and their domination by the European powers led to the
      <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">
       Age of Imperialism
      </a>
      , where European colonial powers came to control most of the planet. The European appetite for trade, commodities, empire and slaves greatly affected many other areas of the world. Spain participated in the destruction of aggressive empires in the Americas, only to substitute its own, and forcibly replaced the original religions. The pattern of territorial aggression was repeated by other European empires, most notably the Dutch, Russian, French and British. Christianity replaced older "pagan" rituals, as were new languages, political and sexual cultures, and in some areas like North America, Australia, New Zealand and
      <a href="/wiki/Argentina" title="Argentina">
       Argentina
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples" title="Indigenous peoples">
       indigenous peoples
      </a>
      were abused and driven off most of their lands, being reduced to small, dependent minorities.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Namban-08.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2390" data-file-width="2629" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Namban-08.jpg/150px-Namban-08.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Namban-08.jpg/225px-Namban-08.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Namban-08.jpg/300px-Namban-08.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Namban-08.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Portuguese
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Gaijin" title="Gaijin">
          Nanbanjin
         </a>
        </i>
        arriving at Japan much to the surprise of locals, detail from
        <a href="/wiki/Nanban_trade" title="Nanban trade">
         Nanban
        </a>
        panel from
        <a href="/wiki/Kan%C5%8D_Domain" title="Kan Domain">
         Kan Domain
        </a>
        , 15931600
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Similarly, in coastal Africa, local states supplied the appetite of European
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_slavery" title="History of slavery">
       slave traders
      </a>
      , changing the complexion of coastal African states and fundamentally altering the nature of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_slave_trade" title="African slave trade">
       African slavery
      </a>
      , causing impacts on societies and economies deep inland. (See
      <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">
       Atlantic slave trade
      </a>
      ).
     </p>
     <p>
      Aboriginal peoples were living in North America at this time and still do today. There were many conflicts between Europeans and Natives. The Europeans had many advantages over the natives. They gave them diseases that they had not been exposed to before and this wiped out 5090% of their population. (See
      <a href="/wiki/Population_history_of_indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Population history of indigenous peoples of the Americas">
       Population history of indigenous peoples of the Americas
      </a>
      .)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125">
       <a href="#cite_note-125">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        125
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">
       Maize
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cassava" title="Cassava">
       manioc
      </a>
      were introduced into Africa in the 16th century by the Portuguese.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126">
       <a href="#cite_note-126">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        126
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      They are now important staple foods, replacing native African crops.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127">
       <a href="#cite_note-127">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        127
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128">
       <a href="#cite_note-128">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        128
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Alfred_W._Crosby" title="Alfred W. Crosby">
       Alfred W. Crosby
      </a>
      speculated that increased production of maize, manioc, and other New World crops led to heavier concentrations of population in the areas from which slavers captured their victims.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129">
       <a href="#cite_note-129">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        129
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the 16th-century economy of China, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">
       Ming Dynasty
      </a>
      was stimulated by trade with the
      <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_Empire" title="Portuguese Empire">
       Portuguese
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">
       Spanish
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Republic" title="Dutch Republic">
       Dutch
      </a>
      . China became involved in a new global trade of goods, plants, animals, and food crops known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Columbian_Exchange" title="Columbian Exchange">
       Columbian Exchange
      </a>
      . Trade with
      <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_Europe" title="Early modern Europe">
       European powers
      </a>
      and the Japanese brought in massive amounts of silver, which then replaced copper and paper
      <a href="/wiki/Banknote" title="Banknote">
       banknotes
      </a>
      as the common
      <a href="/wiki/Medium_of_exchange" title="Medium of exchange">
       medium of exchange
      </a>
      in China. During the last decades of the Ming the flow of silver into China was greatly diminished, thereby undermining state revenues and indeed the entire Ming economy. This damage to the economy was compounded by the effects on agriculture of the incipient
      <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">
       Little Ice Age
      </a>
      , natural calamities, crop failure, and sudden epidemics. The ensuing breakdown of authority and people's livelihoods allowed rebel leaders such as
      <a href="/wiki/Li_Zicheng" title="Li Zicheng">
       Li Zicheng
      </a>
      to challenge
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">
       Ming
      </a>
      authority.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Jesuites_en_chine.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="450" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Jesuites_en_chine.jpg/220px-Jesuites_en_chine.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Jesuites_en_chine.jpg/330px-Jesuites_en_chine.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Jesuites_en_chine.jpg/440px-Jesuites_en_chine.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Jesuites_en_chine.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Jesuit_China_missions" title="Jesuit China missions">
         Jesuit
        </a>
        scholars collaborated extensively with Chinese astronomers, introducing
        <a href="/wiki/Copernican_principle" title="Copernican principle">
         Copernican principles
        </a>
        . Top:
        <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Ricci" title="Matteo Ricci">
         Matteo Ricci
        </a>
        ,
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adam_Schall_von_Bell" title="Adam Schall von Bell">
         Adam Schaal
        </a>
        and
        <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_Verbiest" title="Ferdinand Verbiest">
         Ferdinand Verbiest
        </a>
        (162388); Bottom:
        <a href="/wiki/Xu_Guangqi" title="Xu Guangqi">
         Paul Siu (Xu Guangqi)
        </a>
        ,
        <i>
         Colao
        </i>
        or Prime Minister of State, and his granddaughter Candide Hiu
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      New crops that had come to Asia from the Americas via the Spanish colonizers in the 16th century contributed to the Asia's population growth.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130">
       <a href="#cite_note-130">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        130
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Although the bulk of imports to China were silver, the Chinese also purchased New World crops from the
      <a href="/wiki/Spanish_Empire" title="Spanish Empire">
       Spanish Empire
      </a>
      . This included
      <a href="/wiki/Sweet_potato" title="Sweet potato">
       sweet potatoes
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Maize" title="Maize">
       maize
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Peanut" title="Peanut">
       peanuts
      </a>
      , foods that could be cultivated in lands where traditional Chinese staple cropswheat, millet, and ricecould not grow, hence facilitating a rise in the population of China.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ebrey_2006.2C_p._211_131-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ebrey_2006.2C_p._211-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132">
       <a href="#cite_note-132">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        132
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the Song Dynasty (9601279), rice had become the major staple crop of the poor;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133">
       <a href="#cite_note-133">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        133
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      after sweet potatoes were introduced to China around 1560, it gradually became the traditional food of the lower classes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134">
       <a href="#cite_note-134">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        134
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The arrival of the Portuguese to Japan in 1543 initiated the
      <a href="/wiki/Nanban_trade" title="Nanban trade">
       Nanban trade period
      </a>
      , with the Japanese adopting several technologies and cultural practices, like the
      <a href="/wiki/Arquebus" title="Arquebus">
       arquebus
      </a>
      , European-style cuirasses, European ships, Christianity, decorative art, and language. After the Chinese had banned direct trade by Chinese merchants with Japan, the Portuguese filled this commercial vacuum as intermediaries between China and Japan. The Portuguese bought Chinese silk and sold it to the Japanese in return for Japanese-mined silver; since silver was more highly valued in China, the Portuguese could then use Japanese silver to buy even larger stocks of Chinese silk.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135">
       <a href="#cite_note-135">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        135
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, by 1573after the Spanish established a trading base in Manilathe Portuguese intermediary trade was trumped by the prime source of incoming silver to China from the Spanish Americas.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136">
       <a href="#cite_note-136">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        136
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Italian Jesuit
      <a href="/wiki/Matteo_Ricci" title="Matteo Ricci">
       Matteo Ricci
      </a>
      (15521610), was the first European allowed into the Forbidden City, taught the Chinese how to construct and play the
      <a href="/wiki/Spinet" title="Spinet">
       spinet
      </a>
      , translated Chinese texts into Latin and vice versa, and worked closely with his Chinese associate
      <a href="/wiki/Xu_Guangqi" title="Xu Guangqi">
       Xu Guangqi
      </a>
      (15621633) on mathematical work.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_impact_in_Europe">
       Economic impact in Europe
      </span>
      <span class="mw-editsection">
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        [
       </span>
       <a href="/w/index.php?title=Age_of_Discovery&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Economic impact in Europe">
        edit
       </a>
       <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
        ]
       </span>
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Revolution" title="Commercial Revolution">
       Commercial Revolution
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">
       Renaissance
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_in_the_Low_Countries" title="Renaissance in the Low Countries">
       Renaissance in the Low Countries
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Divergence" title="Great Divergence">
       Great Divergence
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kepler-world.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1056" data-file-width="1776" height="131" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Kepler-world.jpg/220px-Kepler-world.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Kepler-world.jpg/330px-Kepler-world.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/Kepler-world.jpg/440px-Kepler-world.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kepler-world.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        World map from
        <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">
         Johannes Kepler
        </a>
        's
        <a href="/wiki/Rudolphine_Tables" title="Rudolphine Tables">
         Rudolphine Tables
        </a>
        (1627), incorporating many of the new discoveries.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      As a wider variety of global luxury commodities entered the European markets by sea, previous European markets for
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Luxury_good" title="Luxury good">
       luxury goods
      </a>
      stagnated. The Atlantic trade largely supplanted pre-existing
      <a href="/wiki/Maritime_republics" title="Maritime republics">
       Italian
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">
       German
      </a>
      trading powers which had relied on their Baltic, Russian and Islamic trade links. The new commodities also caused
      <a href="/wiki/Social_change" title="Social change">
       social change
      </a>
      , as sugar, spices, silks and chinawares entered the luxury markets of Europe.
     </p>
     <p>
      The European economic center shifted from the Mediterranean to Western Europe. The city of
      <a href="/wiki/Antwerp" title="Antwerp">
       Antwerp
      </a>
      , part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Brabant" title="Duchy of Brabant">
       Duchy of Brabant
      </a>
      , became "the center of the
      <i>
       entire
      </i>
      international economy,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137">
       <a href="#cite_note-137">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        137
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the richest city in Europe at this time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138">
       <a href="#cite_note-138">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        138
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Centered in Antwerp first and then in
      <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">
       Amsterdam
      </a>
      , "
      <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Golden_Age" title="Dutch Golden Age">
       Dutch Golden Age
      </a>
      " was tightly linked to the Age of Discovery.
      <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Guicciardini" title="Francesco Guicciardini">
       Francesco Guicciardini
      </a>
      , a Venetian envoy, stated that hundreds of ships would pass Antwerp in a day, and 2,000 carts entered the city each week. Portuguese ships laden with
      <a href="/wiki/Black_pepper" title="Black pepper">
       pepper
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Cinnamon" title="Cinnamon">
       cinnamon
      </a>
      would unload their cargo. With many foreign merchants resident in the city and governed by an oligarchy of banker-aristocrats forbidden to engage in trade, the economy of Antwerp was foreigner-controlled, which made the city very international, with merchants and traders from
      <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">
       Venice
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Dubrovnik" title="Dubrovnik">
       Ragusa
      </a>
      , Spain and Portugal and a policy of toleration, which attracted a large
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Jew" title="Orthodox Jew">
       Orthodox Jewish
      </a>
      community. The city experienced three booms during its golden age, the first based on the pepper market, a second launched by New World silver coming from Seville (ending with the bankruptcy of Spain in 1557), and a third boom, after the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Italian_War_of_1551%E2%80%931559" title="Italian War of 15511559">
       Treaty of Cateau-Cambresis
      </a>
      , in 1559, based on the textiles industry.
     </p>
     <p>
      Despite initial hostilities, by 1549 the Portuguese were sending annual trade missions to
      <a href="/wiki/Shangchuan_Island" title="Shangchuan Island">
       Shangchuan Island
      </a>
      in China. In 1557 they managed to convince the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ming_Dynasty" title="Ming Dynasty">
       Ming
      </a>
      court to agree on a legal port treaty that would establish
      <a href="/wiki/Macau" title="Macau">
       Macau
      </a>
      as an official Portuguese trade colony.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139">
       <a href="#cite_note-139">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        139
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Portuguese friar
      <a href="/wiki/Gaspar_da_Cruz" title="Gaspar da Cruz">
       Gaspar da Cruz
      </a>
      (c. 1520 February 5, 1570) wrote the first complete book on China and the Ming Dynasty that was published in Europe; it included information on its geography, provinces, royalty, official class, bureaucracy, shipping, architecture, farming, craftsmanship, merchant affairs, clothing, religious and social customs, music and instruments, writing, education, and justice.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140">
       <a href="#cite_note-140">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        140
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DelftChina18thCenturyCompanieDesIndes.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1593" data-file-width="2338" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/DelftChina18thCenturyCompanieDesIndes.jpg/150px-DelftChina18thCenturyCompanieDesIndes.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/DelftChina18thCenturyCompanieDesIndes.jpg/225px-DelftChina18thCenturyCompanieDesIndes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/DelftChina18thCenturyCompanieDesIndes.jpg/300px-DelftChina18thCenturyCompanieDesIndes.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DelftChina18thCenturyCompanieDesIndes.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Delftware" title="Delftware">
         Delftware
        </a>
        depicting Chinese scenes, 18th century.
        <a href="/wiki/Ernest_Cognacq_Museum" title="Ernest Cognacq Museum">
         Ernest Cognacq Museum
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      From China the major exports were
      <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">
       silk
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Porcelain" title="Porcelain">
       porcelain
      </a>
      , adapted to meet European tastes. The
      <a href="/wiki/Chinese_export_porcelain" title="Chinese export porcelain">
       Chinese export porcelains
      </a>
      were held in such great esteem in Europe that, in English,
      <i>
       <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/china" title="wikt:china">
        china
       </a>
      </i>
      became a commonlyused synonym for
      <i>
       porcelain
      </i>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/Kraak_porcelain" title="Kraak porcelain">
       Kraak porcelain
      </a>
      (believed to be named after the Portuguese
      <a href="/wiki/Carrack" title="Carrack">
       carracks
      </a>
      in which it was transported) was among the first Chinese ware to arrive in Europe in mass quantities. Only the richest could afford these early imports, and Kraak often featured in
      <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Golden_Age_painting" title="Dutch Golden Age painting">
       Dutch still life
      </a>
      paintings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141">
       <a href="#cite_note-141">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        141
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Soon the
      <a href="/wiki/Dutch_East_India_Company" title="Dutch East India Company">
       Dutch East India Company
      </a>
      established a lively trade with the East, having imported 6 million porcelain items from China to Europe between the years 1602 to 1682.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142">
       <a href="#cite_note-142">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        142
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143">
       <a href="#cite_note-143">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        143
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Chinese workmanship impressed many. Between 1575 and 1587
      <a href="/wiki/Medici_porcelain" title="Medici porcelain">
       Medici porcelain
      </a>
      from
      <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">
       Florence
      </a>
      was the first successful attempt to imitate Chinese porcelain. Although Dutch potters did not immediately imitate Chinese porcelain, they began to do it when the supply to Europe was interrupted, after the death of
      <a href="/wiki/Wanli_Emperor" title="Wanli Emperor">
       Wanli Emperor
      </a>
      in 1620. Kraak, mainly the
      <a href="/wiki/Blue_and_white_porcelain" title="Blue and white porcelain">
       blue and white porcelain
      </a>
      , was imitated all over the world by potters in
      <a href="/wiki/Arita,_Saga" title="Arita, Saga">
       Arita, Japan
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">
       Persia
      </a>
       where Dutch merchants turned when the fall of the Ming Dynasty rendered Chinese originals unavailable
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144">
       <a href="#cite_note-144">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        144
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and ultimately in
      <a href="/wiki/Delftware" title="Delftware">
       Delftware
      </a>
      . Dutch and later
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/English_Delftware" title="English Delftware">
       English Delftware
      </a>
      inspired by Chinese designs persisted from about 1630 to the mid-18th century alongside European patterns.
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heem,_Jan_Davidsz._de_-_A_Richly_Laid_Table_with_Parrots_-_detail_cup.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="300" data-file-width="300" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Heem%2C_Jan_Davidsz._de_-_A_Richly_Laid_Table_with_Parrots_-_detail_cup.jpg/150px-Heem%2C_Jan_Davidsz._de_-_A_Richly_Laid_Table_with_Parrots_-_detail_cup.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/17/Heem%2C_Jan_Davidsz._de_-_A_Richly_Laid_Table_with_Parrots_-_detail_cup.jpg/225px-Heem%2C_Jan_Davidsz._de_-_A_Richly_Laid_Table_with_Parrots_-_detail_cup.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/17/Heem%2C_Jan_Davidsz._de_-_A_Richly_Laid_Table_with_Parrots_-_detail_cup.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heem,_Jan_Davidsz._de_-_A_Richly_Laid_Table_with_Parrots_-_detail_cup.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Jan_Davidsz._de_Heem" title="Jan Davidsz. de Heem">
         Jan Davidsz. de Heem
        </a>
        , detail of silverware from "A Richly Laid Table with Parrots", c. 1650
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Antonio_de_Morga" title="Antonio de Morga">
       Antonio de Morga
      </a>
      (15591636), a Spanish official in
      <a href="/wiki/Manila" title="Manila">
       Manila
      </a>
      , listed an extensive inventory of goods that were traded by Ming China at the turn of the 16th to 17th century, noting there were "rarities which, did I refer to them all, I would never finish, nor have sufficient paper for it".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145">
       <a href="#cite_note-145">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        145
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      After noting the variety of silk goods traded to Europeans, Ebrey writes of the considerable size of commercial transactions: In one case a galleon to the Spanish territories in the New World carried over 50,000 pairs of silk stockings. In return China imported mostly silver from Peruvian and Mexican mines,
      <a href="/wiki/Manila_galleon" title="Manila galleon">
       transported via Manila
      </a>
      . Chinese merchants were active in these trading ventures, and many emigrated to such places as the
      <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">
       Philippines
      </a>
      and Borneo to take advantage of the new commercial opportunities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ebrey_2006.2C_p._211_131-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ebrey_2006.2C_p._211-131">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        131
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The increase in wealth experienced by
      <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Spain" title="Habsburg Spain">
       Spain
      </a>
      coincided with a major
      <a href="/wiki/Inflation" title="Inflation">
       inflationary
      </a>
      cycle both within Spain and Europe, known as
      <a href="/wiki/Price_revolution" title="Price revolution">
       price revolution
      </a>
      . Spain had amassed large quantities of gold and silver from the New World.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146">
       <a href="#cite_note-146">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        146
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the 1520s large scale extraction of silver from Mexico's
      <a href="/wiki/Guanajuato" title="Guanajuato">
       Guanajuato
      </a>
      began. With the opening of the silver mines in
      <a href="/wiki/Zacatecas" title="Zacatecas">
       Zacatecas
      </a>
      and Bolivia's
      <a href="/wiki/Potos%C3%AD" title="Potos">
       Potos
      </a>
      in 1546 large shipments of silver became the fabled source of wealth. During the 16th century, Spain held the equivalent of US$1.5 trillion (1990 terms) in gold and silver from
      <a href="/wiki/Viceroyalty_of_New_Spain" title="Viceroyalty of New Spain">
       New Spain
      </a>
      . Being the most powerful European monarch at a time full of war and religious conflicts, the
      <a href="/wiki/Habsburg_Spain" title="Habsburg Spain">
       Habsburg
      </a>
      rulers spent the wealth in wars and arts across Europe. "I learnt a proverb here", said a French traveler in 1603: "Everything is dear in Spain except silver".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147">
       <a href="#cite_note-147">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        147
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The spent silver, suddenly spread throughout a previously cash-starved Europe, caused widespread inflation.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148">
       <a href="#cite_note-148">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        148
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The inflation was worsened by a growing population with a static production level, low salaries and a rising cost of living, which damaged local industry. Increasingly, Spain became dependent on the revenues flowing in from the mercantile empire in the Americas, leading to Spain's first bankruptcy in 1557 due to rising military costs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149">
       <a href="#cite_note-149">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        149
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Phillip II of Spain defaulted on debt payments in 1557, 1560, 1575 and 1596. The increase in prices as a result of currency circulation fueled the growth of the commercial
      <a href="/wiki/Middle_class" title="Middle class">
       middle class
      </a>
      in Europe, the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">
        bourgeoisie
       </a>
      </i>
      , which came to influence the politics and culture of many countries.
     </p>

        <span style="display:none;">
        </span>
       </span>
      </li>
      <li>
       <span class="citation book" id="Rice_1970">
        Rice, Eugene, F., Jr ((1970/1994)).
        <i>
         The Foundations of Early Modern Europe: 14601559
        </i>
        . W.W. Norton &amp; Co.
